<｜begin▁of▁sentence｜>You are a mathematical proof generator with an internal proof verifier.

You are given candidate solutions to one problem and their correctness evaluations. Produce one improved proof: combine the strongest ideas, fix the verifier-identified gaps, and prove every claim you use. Verify the result as if grading an IMO solution before finalizing.

Output ONLY the XML format below — no text outside the tags.<｜User｜>Problem:
A line in the plane is called \\emph{sunny} if it is not parallel to any of the $x$-axis, the $y$-axis, or the line $x+y=0$.

Let $n \ge 3$ be a given integer. Determine all nonnegative integers $k$ such that there exist $n$ distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following:
\begin{itemize}
    \item for all positive integers $a$ and $b$ with $a+b\le n+1$, the point $(a,b)$ lies on at least one of the lines; and
    \item exactly $k$ of the $n$ lines are sunny.
\end{itemize}

Candidate solution(s) and correctness evaluation(s):
<candidate id="P13">
<proof>
We determine all possible values of \(k\) for a given integer \(n\ge 3\).

**Construction for \(k=0\).**  
Take the \(n\) vertical lines \(x=i\) for \(i=1,\dots,n\). For any point \((a,b)\in P\) we have \(a\in\{1,\dots,n\}\), so the line \(x=a\) contains \((a,b)\). Thus exactly \(k=0\) sunny lines are used.

**Construction for \(k=1\).**  
Take the \(n-1\) vertical lines \(x=1,\dots,n-1\). They cover all points of \(P\) except \((n,1)\). Add the sunny line through \((n,1)\) with slope \(1\), for instance the line through \((n,1)\) and \((n+1,2)\) (which is different from the vertical lines). This line is sunny because its slope is \(1\notin\{0,\infty,-1\}\). Hence \(k=1\) is possible.

**Construction for \(k=3\).**  
For \(n\ge 3\) take the \(n-3\) vertical lines \(x=1,\dots,n-3\). They cover all points with \(a\le n-3\). Now define three sunny lines:
\[
L_1:\text{ through }(n-2,1)\text{ and }(n-1,2)\quad(\text{slope }1),
\]
\[
L_2:\text{ through }(n-2,2)\text{ and }(n,1)\quad(\text{slope }-\tfrac12),
\]
\[
L_3:\text{ through }(n-2,3)\text{ and }(n-1,1)\quad(\text{slope }-2).
\]
All three slopes are different from \(0,\infty,-1\), so each line is sunny. They cover all points with \(a=n-2,n-1,n\) because
\[
\begin{aligned}
L_1&\text{ contains }(n-2,1)\text{ and }(n-1,2),\\
L_2&\text{ contains }(n-2,2)\text{ and }(n,1),\\
L_3&\text{ contains }(n-2,3)\text{ and }(n-1,1).
\end{aligned}
\]
Thus the whole set \(P\) is covered, and exactly three lines are sunny.

Hence \(k=0,1,3\) are attainable.

**Impossibility of \(k=2\).**  
Assume, for contradiction, that there exist \(n\) distinct lines covering \(P\) with exactly two sunny lines. Let the two sunny lines be \(S_1\) and \(S_2\).

Consider the points with \(a=1\): \((1,1),(1,2),\dots,(1,n)\). A line that is not \(x=1\) can contain at most one of these points, because two points with the same \(x\) would force the line to be vertical \(x=1\). The sunny lines each contain at most one point from this column, so they cover at most two points. Therefore, to cover all \(n\) points, the vertical line \(x=1\) must be one of the non‑sunny lines. Hence \(x=1\) is a non‑sunny line.

Now consider the points with \(a=2\): \((2,1),\dots,(2,n-1)\). The sunny lines cover at most two of them, so at least \(n-3\) points remain. The non‑sunny lines other than \(x=2\) can each contain at most one point from this column. We have \(n-2\) non‑sunny lines in total; one of them is \(x=1\). Thus at most \(n-3\) other non‑sunny lines exist. Consequently, if we do not use \(x=2\) as a non‑sunny line, the maximum number of points from column \(a=2\) that can be covered by all lines is \(2\) (sunny) \(+ (n-3)\) (other non‑sunny) \(= n-1\), which is exactly the number of points. So it is possible in principle to cover this column without \(x=2\), but then every other non‑sunny line must intersect column \(a=2\) in a distinct point, and the two sunny lines must cover the remaining two points.

The diagonal \(s=n+1\) (points \((1,n),(2,n-1),\dots,(n,1)\)) has \(n\) points. The sunny lines can cover at most two of them, so at least \(n-2\) points on this diagonal must be covered by non‑sunny lines. The only non‑sunny line that can cover more than one point of this diagonal is the line \(x+y=n+1\) itself, which covers all \(n\) points. Therefore, to cover the points on \(s=n+1\), it is advantageous to use \(x+y=n+1\) as a non‑sunny line. Assume we do so. Then we have \(n-3\) non‑sunny lines left. The remaining points are those with \(a+b\le n\). Now consider the diagonal \(s=n\). It has \(n-1\) points; the sunny lines cover at most two, so at least \(n-3\) points must be covered by non‑sunny lines. If we use \(x+y=n\) as a non‑sunny line, it covers all \(n-1\) points. Continuing, we could use all diagonals \(s=n+1,n,\dots,4\) as non‑sunny lines. That would be \(n-2\) lines, which is exactly the total number of non‑sunny lines. But then the remaining points are those on \(s=2\) and \(s=3\) (three points: \((1,1),(1,2),(2,1)\)). The two sunny lines would have to cover these three points, which is impossible because any line through two of them is either vertical (\(x=1\)), horizontal (\(y=1\)), or slope \(-1\) (non‑sunny). Hence we cannot use all diagonals \(s=4,\dots,n+1\) as non‑sunny lines.

Therefore, at least one diagonal among \(s=4,\dots,n+1\) is not used as a non‑sunny line. Let \(s=k\) with \(4\le k\le n+1\) be such a diagonal. The points on \(s=k\) are \(k-1\) points. The sunny lines can cover at most two of them, so at least \(k-3\) points must be covered by non‑sunny lines that are not diagonals (i.e., horizontal or vertical lines). Each such non‑sunny line can contain at most one point from this diagonal. Thus we need at least \(k-3\) non‑diagonal non‑sunny lines to cover the points on \(s=k\). However, we have only \(n-2\) non‑sunny lines in total. Among them we have used some diagonals. If we use all diagonals except \(s=k\), that is \(n-3\) diagonals (since there are \(n-2\) diagonals from \(s=4\) to \(n+1\) and we omit one), then we have only \(1\) non‑diagonal non‑sunny line left. This forces \(k-3\le 1\), i.e. \(k\le 4\). Since \(k\ge 4\), we must have \(k=4\). Hence the only possible omitted diagonal is \(s=4\).

Now we have the following set of non‑sunny lines: the diagonals \(s=5,\dots,n+1\) (that is \(n-3\) lines) and one additional non‑diagonal line. Because we already have \(x=1\) as a non‑sunny line (from the first step), the only candidate for the extra non‑diagonal line that can cover points on \(s=4\) is a horizontal line \(y=j\) or a vertical line \(x=i\). But \(x=1\) already covers the first column; if we use \(x=2\) or \(x=3\) as the extra line, we would have used a vertical line that also covers points from other columns, and then the points on \(s=4\) must be covered by the two sunny lines and possibly this line. We analyse the possibilities.

The points on \(s=4\) are \((1,3),(2,2),(3,1)\). The only way to cover all three of them with two sunny lines and one extra line is if the extra line covers one of them, and the two sunny lines together cover the other two. But then the three points on \(s=4\) are covered. The remaining points of \(P\) are those on \(s=2,3\) and also those on \(s=5,\dots,n+1\) that are not covered by the diagonals? Actually we have used the diagonals \(s=5,\dots,n+1\) as non‑sunny lines, so they cover all points with sum at least \(5\). The points with sum \(2\) and \(3\) are not covered by those diagonals, and they also need to be covered. Together with the points on \(s=4\) that are not covered by the extra line, we have a finite set. It can be shown by a case analysis (or by considering the geometry of the triangular grid) that no choice of two sunny lines and one extra horizontal/vertical line can cover all points of \(P\). For a concrete contradiction one may examine the points \((2,1)\) and \((1,3)\) on \(s=3\) and \(s=4\) respectively; they are not collinear with any sunny line that does not also intersect the boundary in a forbidden way. A complete case check for the general \(n\) shows that the only possible extra line is \(y=2\) or \(y=3\) or \(x=2\) or \(x=3\), and then the two sunny lines would have to cover the remaining points, but they cannot because the required slopes would be non‑sunny or the lines would coincide with the already used ones.

Alternatively, a more systematic argument: The points \((1,1),(1,2),(2,1)\) lie on the three smallest diagonals. The two sunny lines can cover at most two of them; the third must be covered by a non‑sunny line. Because \(x=1\) already covers \((1,1)\) and \((1,2)\), the only way to cover \((2,1)\) is by a line through \((2,1)\) that is not horizontal, vertical or slope \(-1\). The only such lines that can also cover other points are the sunny lines. But then the sunny lines must cover the remaining points, and one can show that this leads to a contradiction with the covering of the points on the diagonal \(s=4\). A detailed verification for all \(n\ge 3\) (by induction or by considering the possible positions of the two sunny lines) shows that \(k=2\) is impossible.

Thus the only attainable values are \(k=0,1,3\).

\[
\boxed{k\in\{0,1,3\}}
\]
</proof>

We need to systematically show impossibility. Let's attempt a combinatorial argument using the concept of "sunny lines" and "non-sunny lines" and their intersections.

Maybe we can use the fact that the total number of lines is n, and the points form a triangular array. Consider the points on the hypotenuse a+b = n+1. There are n points on this diagonal. The only lines that can cover more than one point from this diagonal are diagonal lines x+y = constant (slope -1). So if we have a diagonal line, it covers a set of points on that diagonal. But we have n lines total. For the hypotenuse, we have n points. Could we cover them with sunny lines? Each sunny line can intersect a given diagonal at most once (since slope not -1). So the number of points from hypotenuse that can be covered by sunny lines is at most the number of sunny lines (k). So if we have k sunny lines, they can cover at most k points from hypotenuse. The remaining points on hypotenuse must be covered by non-sunny diagonal lines. But we have n-k non-sunny lines, some of which may be diagonal. The diagonal lines that can cover points on hypotenuse are only those with constant s = n+1 (the hypotenuse itself). So if we don't include the diagonal x+y=n+1, then all points on hypotenuse must be covered by sunny lines (each covering at most one). So we would need k ≥ n. Now, if k ≥ 4, then we have at least 4 sunny lines. The total number of points on these lines is at most k * floor((n+1)/2). But we also have non-sunny lines, n-k of them. Another idea: Since the lines are distinct, we can consider their slopes. Sunny lines have slopes not in {0,∞,-1}. So we can think of the set of slopes of the sunny lines as a set of rational numbers not equal to 0,∞,-1. Also, note that if a line has slope m, then the points it contains have the property that the b values are m*a + c. The condition a+b ≤ n+1 gives a(1+m) ≤ n+1-c. So for a given m, the maximum possible a is about (n+1)/ (1+m) if c is chosen appropriately. To maximize the number of points, we want m=1, giving a up to floor((n+1)/2). So indeed the maximum is achieved by slope 1.

Perhaps we can develop a stronger argument: For a given covering with n lines, consider the set of points that are covered by non-sunny lines. Thus we need a more refined argument that shows that if k ≥ 4, then we cannot cover all points because the sunny lines cannot cover the "central" region due to some geometric constraints. Maybe we can use an argument about the "number of lines covering each row" or "each column". For each row b, consider the number of lines that intersect that row. A line that is not horizontal can intersect a given row at most once. A horizontal line y=b intersects row b at all points in that row, i.e., n+1-b points. So if we have a horizontal line y=b, it covers all points in that row. Similarly, a vertical line x=a covers all points in that column. A diagonal line x+y=s covers points in that diagonal.

Given that we have n lines total, we can consider the "covering" of each row. For a given row b, the points are (a,b) with a from 1 to n+1-b. They can be covered by:
- the horizontal line y=b (if present) covers all of them,
- the vertical lines x=a (each covers (a,b) if that line is present) but at most one per vertical line,
- the diagonal lines x+y=s (each covers (a,b) if a+b=s, but at most one per diagonal line),
- sunny lines (each covers at most one point per row, since they are not horizontal).

Thus the number of points in row b that can be covered by non-horizontal lines is at most the number of lines that are not horizontal, but also at most the number of lines that intersect that row. Actually, each line that is not horizontal can contain at most one point from a given row. So if we do not have the horizontal line y=b, then the points in row b must be covered by lines that each contain at most one point from that row. The total number of such lines is n - (number of horizontal lines that are present). But we need to cover all points in row b, which are n+1-b points. So we need n - H_b ≥ n+1-b, where H_b is the number of horizontal lines that are present that could cover row b? Actually, if we have horizontal lines, they cover all points in their row, so they could cover multiple points. But if we do not have y=b, then the covering of row b must come from other lines, each covering at most one point. So the total number of lines that can cover row b is n - (number of horizontal lines that are not b?) Wait, we have n lines total. Some of them are horizontal. Let H be the set of horizontal lines used (non-sunny horizontal lines). For each such line y = d, it covers all points in row d. So for row b, if y=b is in H, then all points in row b are covered by that line, so no need for other lines. If y=b is not in H, then the points in row b must be covered by the remaining lines. Each of those lines can cover at most one point from row b, because they are not horizontal (they could be vertical, diagonal, or sunny). So the number of points in row b is at most the number of lines that are not horizontal (i.e., n - |H|). So we have:
n+1-b ≤ n - |H| if y=b not in H.
=> 1-b ≤ -|H| => |H| ≤ b-1.
Thus if we do not include y=b, then the number of horizontal lines used must be at most b-1. This is a constraint that depends on b.

Similarly, for column a, if x=a is not used, then the number of vertical lines used must be at most a-1.

Also, for diagonal s, if x+y=s is not used, then the number of diagonal lines used must be at most s-2? Actually, diagonal lines x+y=e cover points with sum e. They are non-sunny diagonal lines. A diagonal line x+y=e can cover multiple points from a given diagonal if it is exactly that diagonal. But if we do not have the diagonal line x+y=s, then points on that diagonal must be covered by other lines. Each other line (vertical, horizontal, or sunny) can contain at most one point from that diagonal. So the number of points on diagonal s is s-1. So we need s-1 ≤ n - D, where D is the number of diagonal lines used (non-sunny diagonal lines). That gives D ≤ n - (s-1) = n - s + 1.

These constraints may lead to contradictions when k is large.

Now, we have k sunny lines. So n-k non-sunny lines, which are of three types: vertical (V), horizontal (H), diagonal (D). Let |V| = v, |H| = h, |D| = d, so v+h+d = n-k.

Let's assume we have a covering with k=2. Consider the hypotenuse. If x+y=n+1 is not present, then d=0 and all lines must intersect the hypotenuse. That would mean all lines intersect the hypotenuse at a distinct point. Since we have n lines, they would cover all n points on the hypotenuse. That forces that no line is vertical or horizontal? Actually, a vertical line x=a intersects the hypotenuse at (a, n+1-a) only if that point is in the triangle; so it can intersect the hypotenuse at exactly one point. A horizontal line y=j intersects at (n+1-j, j) only. So they also intersect the hypotenuse. So they are fine. But the condition that all lines intersect the hypotenuse is not restrictive; it just means that each line must contain at least one point on the hypotenuse. That's possible for many lines. However, we also have the other edges. So this line of reasoning might not lead to contradiction.

Perhaps we can use a more global counting: Let's denote the number of lines of each type. For a given covering, consider the total number of "point-line" incidences from the non-sunny lines. Since each such line covers many points, the total incidences is at least the number of points on those lines. But we need to cover all points. The sunny lines cover at most k * floor((n+1)/2) incidences. So the total incidences from all lines is at most (n-k)*n + k*floor((n+1)/2). But we also have that each point must be covered at least once, so the total incidences must be at least T. This gives the earlier inequality, which for n=5 and k=2 gives: 2*3 + 3*5 = 6+15=21 ≥15, okay. For n=6 and k=2: 2*3 + 4*6 = 6+24=30 ≥21, okay. So not contradictory.

Thus we need a stronger necessary condition. Perhaps we can consider that the non-sunny lines cannot cover all points because they are of limited types, and the sunny lines, due to their slopes, cannot cover certain points that are "central". This is reminiscent of the concept of "Sidon sets" and "sum-free" sets.

Let's attempt to prove that k=2 is impossible by contradiction. Suppose we have n lines covering P with exactly 2 sunny lines. Let them be S1 and S2. Consider the points with a=1. There are n points. If x=1 is not among the lines, then the covering of these points must come from other lines. The sunny lines each cover at most one point from column 1 (since they are not vertical). The other lines are non-sunny: horizontal, diagonal, vertical. But vertical lines other than x=1 do not cover column 1. So the only lines that can cover points in column 1 are horizontal and diagonal. So we need that the number of horizontal and diagonal lines is at least n-2? Actually, we have n lines total; if we don't have x=1, then the lines that can cover column 1 are the horizontal lines and the diagonal lines (and the sunny lines). The sunny lines can cover at most 2 points, so they can cover at most 2 points. The remaining n-2 lines are non-sunny. They could be horizontal or diagonal. But horizontal lines can each cover at most one point from column 1 (since they are of the form y=j, they cover (1,j) only). Diagonal lines of the form x+y=s can cover at most one point from column 1 (since they cover (1,s-1) only). So each such line covers at most one point from column 1. Therefore, the maximum number of points in column 1 that can be covered by non-sunny lines is h+d (since each horizontal and diagonal line can cover at most one point from column 1). So we need h+d ≥ n-2 (since sunny lines cover at most 2, total n points). But h+d = n-2 (since V=0 and k=2, total non-sunny lines = n-2, so h+d = n-2). So we need h+d ≥ n-2, which is equality, and also the sunny lines must cover the remaining points? Actually, if h+d = n-2, then the non-sunny lines cover at most n-2 points from column 1 (since each covers at most one). So the sunny lines must cover the remaining at least 2 points. Since there are 2 sunny lines, they can cover at most 2 points from column 1. So they must cover exactly 2 points from column 1. That means each sunny line covers exactly one point from column 1, and the non-sunny lines cover the other n-2 points, each covering a distinct point from column 1. So the union of the intersections of the lines with column 1 is all n points. So all lines intersect column 1 at distinct points. That is possible if the lines are all distinct and none is vertical x=1 (since that would cover all points). So this scenario is not contradictory. So we cannot rule out that x=1 is not present.

Now, similarly, consider row 1. If y=1 is not present, then we need h=0? Actually, from earlier, if y=1 not present, then to cover row 1, we need H=0 (no horizontal lines). Because if there are any horizontal lines, they cover some rows; but y=1 not present means that the horizontal line y=1 is not used. The other horizontal lines y=j (j≠1) do not cover row 1. So the covering of row 1 must come from non-horizontal lines (vertical, diagonal, sunny). The number of such lines is n - H. Since H is the total number of horizontal lines, which could be non-zero. But we have no horizontal lines if y=1 not present? Wait, the condition we derived earlier: For row 1, if y=1 is not present, then we need H ≤ 0? Actually, from the inequality: n ≤ n - H => H ≤ 0. So if y=1 is not present, then H must be 0. That is, no horizontal lines at all. So if y=1 is not present, then H=0. So either y=1 is present, or H=0. Similarly, for column 1, if x=1 not present, then V=0. So we have:
- Either x=1 is present, or V=0.
- Either y=1 is present, or H=0.
- Either x+y=n+1 is present, or D=0.

Now, suppose we have k=2. Then we have n-2 non-sunny lines. We can try to see if it's possible that none of the three special lines are present. Then we must have V=0, H=0, D=0, so all non-sunny lines are absent, meaning n-2 = 0 => n=2, but n≥3. So at least one special line must be present. Possibly more than one.

Now, consider the point (1,1). It can be covered by x=1, y=1, or the diagonal x+y=2. If we have x=1 present, then (1,1) is covered. If we have y=1 present, then covered. If we have x+y=2 present, then covered. So (1,1) is covered in any case if any of these lines are present. But we need to cover all points.

Maybe we can show that if k=2, then the covering of the triangle forces that the two sunny lines must intersect the hypotenuse at points that are "far apart", leading to a contradiction with covering of the first column. I think we need to use a more refined counting that considers the number of points on the "interior" that can be covered by sunny lines given the constraints from the edges.

Another idea: Use an "Lattice" argument. For each line L, consider the set of integer points on L within P. The lines are distinct. For a sunny line, the set of points it contains is a set of points with distinct a,b,a+b. This set can be thought of as a "permutation" in the triangular array. The maximum size of such a set is floor((n+1)/2). So the number of points on a sunny line is at most that. Now, consider the three "special" lines: x=1, y=1, x+y=n+1. They each cover many points. If we have few non-sunny lines, then many points must be covered by sunny lines. The total number of points that are not covered by any non-sunny line is at least something. We can try to bound the number of points that can be covered by sunny lines given that they are distinct and have slopes not 0,∞,-1. Perhaps we can use the fact that the union of the sunny lines covers a set of points that must be a "Sidon set" in the grid? Not exactly.

Given the time, I think it's best to adopt a known solution approach. I recall that in the IMO Shortlist 2007 or something, there is a problem about covering points with lines and sunny lines. The answer is {0,1,3}. The solution involves showing that k cannot be 2 or ≥4. For k=2, one can argue by considering the points with a=1 and b=1 and a+b=n+1, and using the pigeonhole principle on the slopes of the two sunny lines.

Let's try to prove k=2 impossible directly.

Assume there exist n distinct lines covering P with exactly 2 sunny lines, call them L and M. Consider the points on the vertical line a=1. They are (1,1),(1,2),...,(1,n). The only lines that can cover more than one of them are vertical lines x=1. If x=1 is not among the lines, then each of the n lines must cover a distinct point from this column. In particular, the two sunny lines each must intersect the vertical line a=1 at some points. Since there are n points, we have a bijection between lines and points on column 1. That means that for each line, its intersection with a=1 is a unique point. So the lines are all distinct and each passes through a different point of column 1. Now, consider the points on the horizontal line b=1. They are (1,1),(2,1),...,(n,1). Similarly, if y=1 is not among the lines, then each line must intersect b=1 at a distinct point. So we have two sets of intersections: each line must intersect both a=1 and b=1 at distinct points. This is possible if the lines have slopes that are not 0 or ∞.

Now, consider the point (1,1). It is the intersection of a=1 and b=1. It must be covered by some line. If x=1 is not present, then the vertical line x=1 is not used. If y=1 is not present, then the horizontal line y=1 is not used. So (1,1) could be covered by the diagonal line x+y=2 (which is a non-sunny line) or by a sunny line. Since we have two sunny lines, they could pass through (1,1). But if they do, then they cover (1,1). That's fine.

Now, consider the point (n,1) on the horizontal edge. It is also on the hypotenuse? Since a=n, b=1, sum = n+1, so it's on the hypotenuse if n+1 = n+1, yes. So (n,1) is on the hypotenuse. Similarly, (1,n) is on the hypotenuse. So the hypotenuse points are (1,n),(2,n-1),...,(n,1). The only lines that can cover multiple points on the hypotenuse are the diagonal line x+y=n+1. If that line is not present, then each of the n points on the hypotenuse must be covered by lines that each intersect the hypotenuse at most once (since they are not the hypotenuse line). So we need at least n lines to cover the hypotenuse. Since we have exactly n lines, that forces each line to intersect the hypotenuse at a distinct point. So all lines must intersect the hypotenuse. That means that the two sunny lines must each intersect the hypotenuse at some point. Also, the non-sunny lines (which are vertical, horizontal, or diagonal) must also intersect the hypotenuse. But note: A vertical line x=a intersects the hypotenuse at (a, n+1-a). So if there is any vertical line other than x=1, it will intersect the hypotenuse. However, if we have a vertical line x=a, it also intersects column 1? No, it does not. But we already have that all lines must intersect column 1 if x=1 is not present. So if we have a vertical line x=a (a>1), it does not intersect column 1, which would violate the condition that all lines intersect column 1 (since we need each line to cover a distinct point from column 1). Therefore, if we try to cover the hypotenuse without the diagonal line, we would need all lines to intersect the hypotenuse, which forces that no line is vertical x=a for a>1? Actually, vertical lines do intersect the hypotenuse, so they are fine. But then they also must intersect column 1. A vertical line x=a does not intersect column 1 (a=1) unless a=1. So to have all lines intersect column 1, we cannot have any vertical line other than x=1. So if x=1 is not present, we cannot have any vertical lines. So v=0. Then the non-sunny lines are all horizontal or diagonal. They must also intersect the hypotenuse. Horizontal lines y=j intersect the hypotenuse at (n+1-j, j). So they do intersect. Diagonal lines x+y=s intersect the hypotenuse at (s? Actually, if s ≠ n+1, they intersect the hypotenuse at most once? The line x+y=s intersects the hypotenuse x+y=n+1 at no points if s≠n+1. So if we have a diagonal line other than x+y=n+1, it does not intersect the hypotenuse. So to have all lines intersect the hypotenuse, we cannot have any diagonal lines except x+y=n+1. So if we don't have x+y=n+1, we cannot have any diagonal lines. That forces d=0. Then the non-sunny lines are all horizontal. But we also need to cover column 1, and we have no vertical lines, so we need all lines to intersect column 1. Horizontal lines do intersect column 1 at (1,j). So they are fine. So we could have h = n-2, and all lines are either horizontal or sunny. But then we also need to cover row 1? Let's analyze this possibility: Suppose we have no x=1, no y=1, no x+y=n+1. Then we must have v=0, h=0, d=0, so all lines are sunny. That would be k=n. So if k=2, we cannot have all three special lines absent. So at least one special line must be present. This leads to different cases.

Let's do a systematic case analysis based on which of the three special lines (x=1, y=1, x+y=n+1) are present. We have k=2 sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are n-2 lines. They can be vertical, horizontal, diagonal. We need to cover all points.

Define:
- V: number of vertical non-sunny lines.
- H: number of horizontal non-sunny lines.
- D: number of diagonal non-sunny lines.
So V+H+D = n-2.

Constraints:
1. If x=1 is not present, then V=0. (Because otherwise, if there is a vertical line x=i, it would be a non-sunny line, and it would not cover column 1 points; but we need to cover column 1 points. If x=1 is not present, we need all lines to intersect column 1 at distinct points. If there is any vertical line x=i (i>1), it does not intersect column 1, so that would be impossible. So V must be 0.)
2. If y=1 is not present, then H=0.
3. If x+y=n+1 is not present, then D=0.

So we have the following possibilities for the presence of the special lines:

Case A: x=1 is present, y=1 is present, x+y=n+1 is present. Then we have at least 3 non-sunny lines (the special ones). So V≥1, H≥1, D≥1. Since V+H+D = n-2, we have n-2 ≥ 3 => n≥5. For n=5, n-2=3, so we could have exactly these three lines. That would give V=1, H=1, D=1. For n=6, n-2=4, so we could have V=1, H=1, D=1 and one extra non-sunny line. That's possible. So we need to check if such a covering can exist with k=2. Let's attempt to see if we can cover all points with these lines.

For n=5, we have lines: x=1, y=1, x+y=6. The points covered by these three lines: x=1 covers (1,1)-(1,5); y=1 covers (1,1)-(5,1); x+y=6 covers (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). So distinct points: from x=1: 5 points (1,1)-(1,5); from y=1: additional points (2,1),(3,1),(4,1) = 3 points; from x+y=6: additional points (2,4),(3,3),(4,2) = 3 points (since (1,5) and (5,1) already counted). Total distinct = 5+3+3=11. The triangle has 15 points. The remaining 4 points are those not on these lines: (2,2),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1)? Wait, (4,1) is on y=1 and x+y=5? So impossible. Therefore, for n=5, the covering with x=1, y=1, x+y=6 and 2 sunny lines fails. Could we use a different combination of non-sunny lines? For n=5, we have only 3 non-sunny lines total (since n-k=3). The only way to have 3 non-sunny lines is to use the three special lines? Possibly we could use some other lines, but then the constraints apply. For example, we could have x=1, y=1, and some other vertical/horizontal/diagonal line instead of the hypotenuse. Assume we have a covering with exactly 2 sunny lines. Then there are n-2 non-sunny lines. We know that x+y=n+1 must be among them (to cover the hypotenuse). So D includes that line. Now, consider the vertical edge. If x=1 is not present, then we need V=0, so all vertical lines must be absent. That means any non-sunny vertical line would be a problem. So we can choose to have x=1 present or not. Similarly for y=1.

Let's attempt to find a potential covering. Suppose we include x=1 and y=1 as non-sunny lines. Then we have at least 3 non-sunny lines: x=1, y=1, x+y=8. That gives V=1, H=1, D=1. Then we have n-3 non-sunny lines left. For n=7, n-3=4 extra non-sunny lines. So total non-sunny lines = 5 (n-2=5). So we need to add 2 more non-sunny lines (since V+H+D=1+1+1=3, and total non-sunny =5, so we need 2 more). Those could be vertical, horizontal, or diagonal. But we already have x=1 and y=1, and x+y=8. Additional vertical lines: x=2,3,4,5,6,7; but if we add x=2, then for column 1, we have x=1 present, so V=2. That's fine. Additional horizontal lines: y=2,3,4,5,6,7; similarly. Additional diagonal lines: x+y=s for s=2,...,7, but note that s=8 is already used. Could use s=7,6,5,4,3,2. However, if we add a diagonal line x+y=7, it will cover points with sum 7, which are (1,6),(2,5),(3,4),(4,3),(5,2),(6,1). That might help cover some points.

Now, the uncovered points from the three special lines (x=1, y=1, x+y=8) are the points with a+b ≤7? Actually the triangle for n=7 includes sums 2..8. The three lines cover:
x=1: (1,1)-(1,7)
y=1: (1,1)-(7,1)
x+y=8: (1,7),(2,6),(3,5),(4,4),(5,3),(6,2),(7,1)
So distinct points: from x=1:7 points; from y=1: additional 6 points (since (1,1) counted, so 6 new); from x+y=8: additional 5 points (since (1,7) and (7,1) already counted, so new: (2,6),(3,5),(4,4),(5,3),(6,2) — that's 5). Total 7+6+5=18. The triangle has 28 points, so 10 points remain. Those points are: all points with sum ≤7 that are not on x=1 or y=1? Let's list all points by sum:
sum2: (1,1) covered.
sum3: (1,2),(2,1) covered.
sum4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) covered? (1,3) in x=1; (2,2) not on x=1 or y=1 or x+y=8? (2,2) sum=4; (3,1) in y=1. So (2,2) uncovered.
sum5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) covered? (1,4) x=1; (2,3) not; (3,2) not; (4,1) y=1. So (2,3),(3,2) uncovered.
sum6: (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1) covered? (1,5) x=1; (2,4) not; (3,3) not; (4,2) not; (5,1) y=1. So (2,4),(3,3),(4,2) uncovered.
sum7: (1,6),(2,5),(3,4),(4,3),(5,2),(6,1) covered? (1,6) x=1; (2,5) not; (3,4) not; (4,3) not; (5,2) not; (6,1) y=1. So (2,5),(3,4),(4,3),(5,2) uncovered.
sum8: (1,7),(2,6),(3,5),(4,4),(5,3),(6,2),(7,1) covered by x=1,y=1, or x+y=8.
So uncovered points: (2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(2,5),(3,2),(3,3),(3,4),(4,2),(4,3),(4,? Actually (4,2) uncovered, (4,3) uncovered, (5,2) uncovered. That's 10 points. So we need to cover these 10 points with the 2 sunny lines and the remaining 2 non-sunny lines (since total non-sunny =5, we already have 3, so 2 more). The sunny lines can cover at most 4 points each (since floor(8/2)=4). So total max incidences from sunny lines =8, which is less than 10, but we also have the 2 non-sunny lines which can cover additional points. However, we need to cover each point at least once. The 2 non-sunny lines can be chosen to cover some of these points. Let's see if we can choose the 2 extra non-sunny lines to cover the remaining points after the sunny lines. The sunny lines can cover up to 8 points. So we would need at least 2 points covered by the extra non-sunny lines. That's possible if the sunny lines cover 8 of the 10 points, leaving 2 to be covered by the non-sunny lines. But we also need to consider that the non-sunny lines must be distinct from the special ones. They could be vertical lines x=2,3,...; horizontal lines y=2,...; or diagonal lines x+y=s. However, if we choose a vertical line x=2, it will cover points (2,1)-(2,6) within the triangle. But (2,1) is already covered by y=1, (2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(2,5),(2,6) are among the uncovered points? That would be 4 points. But x=2 is a vertical line, which is non-sunny. So we could use that. Similarly, horizontal y=2 covers (1,2),(2,2),(3,2),(4,2),(5,2),(6,2) — (1,2) already covered, (2,2),(3,2),(4,2),(5,2) are uncovered, (6,2) on hypotenuse? (6,2) sum=8, already covered. So y=2 would cover 4 uncovered points. Diagonal x+y=7 covers (1,6),(2,5),(3,4),(4,3),(5,2),(6,1) — (1,6) already covered, (2,5),(3,4),(4,3),(5,2) are uncovered, (6,1) already covered. So that covers 4 points. So we could potentially use two such lines to cover many of the uncovered points. However, we also have the sunny lines. The problem is that the sunny lines must be distinct from these non-sunny lines, and they have slopes not 0,∞,-1. Could we cover all 10 points with 2 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines? Let's try to see if it's possible.

The uncovered set: U = {(2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(2,5),(3,2),(3,3),(3,4),(4,2),(4,3),(5,2)}. Note that (2,2) and (2,3) are on the same vertical line x=2. (2,2) and (3,2) are on same horizontal y=2. (2,5) and (3,4) are on line x+y=7? Actually (2,5) sum=7, (3,4) sum=7, so they are on x+y=7. So many collinearities.

Now, suppose we try to cover U with two sunny lines and two non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines could be chosen as x=2 and y=2, which would cover: x=2 covers (2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(2,5) (since (2,1) and (2,6) already covered). y=2 covers (3,2),(4,2),(5,2) (since (1,2) already covered, (2,2) already covered by x=2, (6,2) already covered). (3,4) and (4,3) have slope -1, line x+y=7, non-sunny. So no sunny line can contain any two of these three points. Therefore, we need at least three sunny lines to cover them, but we have only two. What if we choose the two non-sunny lines as x=2 and x+y=7? x=2 covers (2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(2,5). x+y=7 covers (2,5),(3,4),(4,3),(5,2) (since (1,6) and (6,1) already covered). If we choose y=2 and x+y=7, similar. If we choose x=2 and y=2, we already did. So the uncovered points are many, and the two extra non-sunny lines can't cover all without leaving a small set that needs more sunny lines. What if we use a different combination? Perhaps we don't include x=1 and y=1? Let's try a scenario where we have only x+y=8 as a non-sunny line, and the other non-sunny lines are vertical/horizontal but not x=1,y=1. We attempted with x=2 and y=2, leaving 3 points uncovered, and we needed 2 sunny lines to cover them, impossible. Could we use other non-sunny lines to cover all 10? For instance, use x=2 and x+y=7? Thus it seems that for n=7, k=2 is impossible. More generally, we can argue that if k=2, then the uncovered points after including the hypotenuse diagonal and the other necessary non-sunny lines form a set that cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines because the only possible collinearities within that set are vertical, horizontal, or slope -1, which are all non-sunny. So the maximum number of points a sunny line can cover from that set is 1? Actually, we need to prove that any line with slope not 0,∞,-1 can contain at most 1 point from that set? But we saw that in some cases, a sunny line can contain 2 points from the uncovered set, but not enough to cover all.

Maybe we can prove that the set of points not covered by the special lines is a "staircase" shape, and any sunny line can contain at most 1 point from it? Let's examine the uncovered set for n=7: U = {(2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(2,5),(3,2),(3,3),(3,4),(4,2),(4,3),(5,2)}. The points in U are not all in distinct rows and columns? A line with slope m not 0,∞,-1 can contain at most 1 point from each row? Actually, if the line is not horizontal, it can intersect each row at most once. So it can contain at most 1 point from each row. So it can contain at most as many points as the number of distinct rows it passes through. In U, the rows are 2,3,4,5. So a sunny line could contain at most 4 points from U if it passes through all these rows. But can it pass through all these rows? For a line with slope m, the y values are m*a + c. Since a ranges from 1 to n, the points on the line have y values that are linear in a. The set of rows (y) that appear are determined by the line. For a given sunny line, it can contain many points from U if it passes through the appropriate columns. But we need to check if a sunny line can contain 2 points from U. For example, the line through (2,2) and (3,3) has slope 1, and it contains (2,2) and (3,3) both in U. So it contains 2 points. Could it contain 3? The points in U with a=b are (2,2),(3,3) but (4,4) not in U, (1,1) not in U, so only 2. So slope 1 gives at most 2. For slope 2, points (a,2a+c) with c integer. In U, (2,4) would be on line if c=0? For three points to be collinear on a line with slope not 0,∞,-1, the rows must be distinct, and the columns must be distinct. In U, the rows are 2,3,4,5. So a line could pass through rows 2,3,4,5. (2,4),(3,3),(4,2) on slope -1, not sunny. (2,5),(3,4),(4,3) on slope -1, not sunny. (2,2),(3,4) slope? (4-2)/(3-2)=2, so line through (2,2) and (3,4) has slope 2; does it contain (4,6)? Not in U. So no three. It seems that no sunny line can contain 3 points from U because the only lines that could contain 3 points are those that are vertical/horizontal/slope -1, which are non-sunny. So each sunny line covers at most 2 points from U.

Now, if we have k=2 sunny lines, they can cover at most 4 points from U. But U has 10 points, so we need to cover the remaining 6 points with the extra non-sunny lines (if any). Now, for larger n, we need a general argument. The pattern is that if we have k=2, then we must include the hypotenuse diagonal. Now, we have a covering with k=2 sunny lines. We already know that to cover the hypotenuse (sum=n+1), we must include the diagonal x+y=n+1. That line covers all points on the hypotenuse. So the set of points that must be covered by other lines is the set of points with sum ≤ n. The points with sum=n+1 are already covered. Thus for n=7, k=2 impossible. For larger n, the pattern likely persists: the interior points U have size (n-2)(n-3)/2, and each sunny line can cover at most something like floor((n-2)/2) points from U? Actually, the maximum number of points from U on a line with slope not 0,∞,-1 is at most floor((n-2)/2)? Let's find the maximum possible for general n. The line y = x + d: points (a,a+d) with a≥2, a+d≥2, 2a+d ≤ n. The number of integer solutions a,d is the number of a such that a≥2 and a+d≥2 and d ≤ n-2a. For a given a, d can range from 2-a to n-2a. The extra non-sunny lines, each can cover at most n-3 points from U, so with n-5 extra lines, they can cover at most (n-5)(n-3) points. For large n, that's about n^2, so it's possible. But the problem is that the points in U are arranged in a triangular grid; the non-sunny lines that cover many points from U are those that are vertical, horizontal, or diagonal (slope -1). These lines, when chosen, cover a "strip" of points. The points that are left after using these lines might not be coverable by sunny lines because they are "staircase" points that are not collinear with a sunny slope.

Maybe we can use a counting argument based on the fact that the set U has a "rank" property. Consider the matrix of points versus lines. Each point must be covered. The lines are of three types. The key is to show that if we have k=2, then the total number of incidences from sunny lines is at most something, and the non-sunny lines can only cover a certain number of points, leading to a contradiction with the total number of points.

This is a combinatorial design problem; we need to show that for k=4, it's impossible. The candidate solution likely argued that the only way to cover all points is to use exactly 3 non-sunny lines (the special ones) and 3 sunny lines, or 0 non-sunny lines and 3 sunny lines, etc. They concluded k∈{0,1,3}. Given the time, I think the best approach is to adopt a known solution: Use double counting on the number of points on each line type, and then use the constraints from the three edges to show that k must be 0,1,3. I can write a rigorous proof based on the following ideas:

1. Show that if k ≥ 4, then we must have x=1, y=1, and x+y=n+1 as non-sunny lines, because otherwise we would need at least n sunny lines to cover the boundary points (since each sunny line can cover at most one point from each of the three boundaries). Actually, we need to prove that to cover the vertical edge, we need either x=1 or at least n sunny lines. But we have k sunny lines, which is < n if k < n. So for k < n, we must have x=1. Similarly, for y=1, and for the hypotenuse, we must have x+y=n+1. So for k < n, we have these three lines. For k = n, we have no non-sunny lines, and we need to cover all points with sunny lines. But we can show that for n≥4, it's impossible because the sum of the maximum points per line is less than the number of points (since each sunny line can cover at most floor((n+1)/2) points, and n * floor((n+1)/2) = n(n+1)/2 for n odd, but for n even, it's n(n)/2 = n^2/2, while the triangle has n(n+1)/2 points, which is larger for n≥4? For n=4, n(n+1)/2=10, n * floor(5/2)=4*2=8 <10. For n=5, 15, 5*3=15, so it's exactly equal. But we found that for n=5, all sunny lines would need to cover each point exactly once, but we cannot because the only line with 3 points is y=x, which covers only 3 points; other lines cover at most 2 points. So it's impossible. For n=6, 21, 6*3=18 <21. So k=n impossible for n≥4. So k cannot be n for n≥4. For n=3, k=3 works. So for n≥4, k ≤ n-1.

2. For k ≤ n-1, we must have x=1, y=1, x+y=n+1 as non-sunny lines. So there are at least 3 non-sunny lines. Then k = n - 3 - d, where d is the number of additional non-sunny lines (since total non-sunny = n-k). Actually, we have v+h+d ≥ 3 (the special ones), and total non-sunny = n-k. So n-k ≥ 3 => k ≤ n-3. Thus k ≤ n-3. So for n≥4, k can be at most n-3. But we need to determine all possible k, not just upper bound.

3. Now, consider the points in the interior of the triangle after removing the three special lines. The interior points are U = {(a,b): a,b ≥ 2, a+b ≤ n}. The number of such points is (n-2)(n-3)/2. These points must be covered by the remaining non-sunny lines (if any) and the sunny lines. The remaining non-sunny lines are n-k-3 lines (since we used 3 special ones). For k=0, we have n non-sunny lines, but we need to cover the triangle; k=0 means all lines are non-sunny, and the construction with all vertical lines works. So k=0 possible. For k=1, we have 1 sunny line and n-1 non-sunny lines. The three special lines might not all be present; we could have, say, x=1 and y=1 and some other non-sunny line instead of the hypotenuse, but then the hypotenuse points need to be covered. With 1 sunny line, we cannot cover the hypotenuse if we don't have the diagonal line, because we would need at least n sunny lines. So we must include the hypotenuse diagonal. So we have x=1,y=1,x+y=n+1 as non-sunny lines. Then we have n-1 non-sunny lines total; we already used 3, so we have n-4 extra non-sunny lines. For n=4, n-4=0, so no extra lines; we have 1 sunny line to cover U of size (2*1)/2=1. That works because we can have a sunny line through the single point (2,2) with slope 1? Actually, for n=4, U = {(2,2)}. A sunny line through (2,2) with slope 1 (y=x) contains (2,2) and (1,1) but (1,1) already covered, so it's fine. So k=1 works. For n=5, U = {(2,2),(2,3),(3,2)}. We have 1 sunny line and 2 extra non-sunny lines. The construction given for k=1 uses only one vertical line (x=1) and one sunny line? Actually, the construction for k=1: take n-1 vertical lines x=1,...,n-1. That gives 0 sunny lines. So for k=1, we need a different construction. So for n=5, we have vertical lines x=1,2,3,4 (4 lines) and sunny line through (5,1) with slope 1. That covers all points. So in that configuration, we do not include y=1 or x+y=6 as lines. So the special lines are not all present. So our earlier deduction that for k < n, we must have x=1 and y=1 and x+y=n+1 is not necessarily true if we have enough sunny lines to cover the boundaries? Wait, we argued that if x=1 is not present, then we need v=0 (no vertical lines) and all lines must intersect column 1. So row 1 points are covered by vertical lines. So the inequality n ≤ n - H is not valid if we have vertical lines that cover row 1 points. In the earlier derivation, we considered covering row 1: points (1,1) to (n,1). We said that if y=1 is not present, then to cover these points, we need non-horizontal lines (vertical, diagonal, sunny) each covering at most one point from that row. That gives n ≤ number of non-horizontal lines, which is n - H. That inequality is correct if we assume that the only lines that can cover points on row 1 are non-horizontal lines. But vertical lines and diagonal lines and sunny lines are non-horizontal, so they can cover at most one point from row 1. So the inequality is n ≤ n - H => H ≤ 0. That forces H=0. But that inequality holds only if we cannot cover any point on row 1 with a horizontal line. If we have horizontal line y=1, that would cover all row 1 points. But if we don't have y=1, then we must cover row 1 with non-horizontal lines. That is correct. However, in the construction for k=1, we have vertical lines x=1,...,n-1. These vertical lines cover points on row 1: (1,1) from x=1, (2,1) from x=2, ..., (n-1,1) from x=n-1. That's n-1 points. The point (n,1) is covered by the sunny line. So all row 1 points are covered. The number of non-horizontal lines is n-1 (vertical lines) + 1 (sunny line) = n lines? Actually total lines: n-1 vertical lines + 1 sunny line = n lines. The horizontal lines are 0. So H=0, which satisfies H≤0. So the condition is satisfied. So in this construction, we do not have y=1, but H=0, so the inequality n ≤ n - H gives n ≤ n, which is equality. So H=0 is allowed. So the deduction that we must have y=1 is not forced; we can have H=0 and still cover row 1 with non-horizontal lines. But the inequality we used earlier was n ≤ n - H, which gives H ≤ 0. So H=0 is allowed. So we don't need y=1. So the earlier step that "for k < n, we must include x=1, y=1, x+y=n+1" is not correct; we only need that the total number of non-horizontal lines is at least n to cover row 1. If H=0, then we have n non-horizontal lines, which is exactly the number of points on row 1. So it's possible. Thus the necessary condition is not that these special lines must be present, but that we must have enough lines of the appropriate types to cover the boundaries.

Therefore, the approach of forcing the special lines is not always valid. We need a more systematic argument.

Given the complexity, I think the best is to adopt a known solution from the IMO Shortlist. I recall that the problem is from IMO 2007 Shortlist or something. Thus the general approach: For k=2, we need to show that it's impossible. The candidate solution attempted to do so by analyzing the diagonal sums. I think we can write a rigorous proof using a double counting argument on the number of points on each line, combined with the fact that the only lines that can cover many points are those with slopes 0,∞,-1, and that these lines cannot be too many because the total number of lines is limited.

Given the time, I'll produce a proof that is as rigorous as possible, based on the candidate's ideas but fixing the gaps. I'll structure the proof as follows:

- Define the set P.
- For each line L, define its "type" and count.
- Show that if k ≥ 4, then we must have the three special lines x=1, y=1, x+y=n+1, and then use an inequality on the size of U to show that k cannot be ≥4.
- Show that if k = 2, then we must also have these three lines, and then use a combinatorial argument on the points in U to show that they cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines and the remaining non-sunny lines.
- For k = 0 and k = 1, provide explicit constructions.

I'll attempt to make the argument rigorous:

Lemma 1: In any covering of P with n lines, let v, h, d, s be the numbers of vertical, horizontal, diagonal, and sunny lines. Then v+h+d+s = n. Moreover, if s < n, then we must have v ≥ 1 (or else we cannot cover the vertical edge), etc. Actually, we need to derive constraints.

Consider the points with a=1. There are n such points. The only lines that can contain more than one of these points are vertical lines x=1. So if x=1 is not among the lines, then each of the n lines must contain at most one point from this column. Therefore, to cover all n points, we need at least n lines that are not vertical. Since we have exactly n lines, this forces that no line is vertical. So V=0. Similarly, if y=1 is not among the lines, then H=0. If x+y=n+1 is not among the lines, then D=0.

Thus, for any covering, if we want to avoid using x=1, we must have V=0; if we want to avoid using y=1, we must have H=0; if we want to avoid using x+y=n+1, we must have D=0.

In general, for n≥6, the total points to be covered by sunny lines is |U| + (2s-1) = (n-2)(n-3)/2 + 3. For n=6, that's 6+3=9; for n=7, 10+3=13; for n=8, 15+3=18. The maximum coverage by 2 sunny lines is 2 * floor((n+1)/2). For n even, 2 * (n/2) = n; for n odd, 2 * ((n+1)/2) = n+1. So we need (n-2)(n-3)/2 + 3 ≤ n+1 for n odd, or ≤ n for n even. For n=6 (even): (4*3)/2+3=6+3=9 >6. For n=7 (odd): (5*4)/2+3=10+3=13 >8 (since n+1=8). So inequality fails. For n=5: (3*2)/2+3=3+3=6, and 2 * floor(6/2)=2*3=6, equality. So it's possible only if the sunny lines cover exactly all points, which we saw is impossible due to geometry. For n=4: (2*1)/2+3=1+3=4, and 2 * floor(5/2)=2*2=4, equality. But we already have a construction for k=0 and k=1 for n=4. So k=2 is impossible for n=4 as well. So k=2 impossible for all n≥3.

Thus we can write a rigorous argument for k=2:

Assume there exists a covering with exactly 2 sunny lines. Then there are n-2 non-sunny lines. Consider the vertical edge a=1. If the vertical line x=1 is not present, then we must have no vertical lines (V=0). Similarly, for the horizontal edge, if y=1 is not present, then H=0. For the hypotenuse, if x+y=n+1 is not present, then D=0. Since we have only 2 sunny lines, if we try to avoid all three special lines, we would have V=H=D=0, so all lines are sunny, contradiction. So at least one special line must be present. Suppose exactly one special line is present. Without loss, assume x=1 is present, and y=1 and x+y=n+1 are absent. Then as argued, we must have V≥1 (x=1 is vertical) and H=0, D=0. So the non-sunny lines are all vertical. Let V = n-2 (since total non-sunny = n-2, all vertical). Then we have vertical lines covering n-2 columns, including x=1. The remaining columns (those not covered by vertical lines) are exactly the columns a = V+1,...,n, which are n-(V) = 2 columns. The points on the horizontal edge that are not covered by vertical lines are (V+1,1) and (V+2,1) if V+2 ≤ n. That's 2 points. The points on the hypotenuse not covered by vertical lines are (V+1, n-V) and (V+2, n-V-1) if these are in the triangle. That's also 2 points. However, note that (V+2,1) is the point (n,1) if V+2=n, and (V+2, n-V-1) = (n,1) as well, so the sets overlap. The total distinct boundary points not covered by vertical lines is 3. These three points must be covered by the two sunny lines. Each sunny line can cover at most one of these points because a line that is not horizontal can cover at most one point from a horizontal edge; a line that is not diagonal can cover at most one point from the hypotenuse; a line that is not vertical can cover at most one point from the vertical edge. So a sunny line can cover at most one boundary point from each of these edges. But the three points are on two edges: two are on the horizontal edge (the two points with b=1), and one is on the hypotenuse (the point (n,1) is on both, but that's the same point). Actually, the three distinct points are: (V+1,1) on horizontal edge; (V+2,1) on horizontal edge (and also on hypotenuse if V+2=n); (V+1, n-V) on hypotenuse. So we have two points on the horizontal edge and one on the hypotenuse. A sunny line can cover at most one point from the horizontal edge and at most one from the hypotenuse, so it can cover at most 2 boundary points. With 2 sunny lines, we can cover at most 4 boundary points, but we only have 3 to cover, so that's fine. However, we also need to cover the interior points of the triangle that are not on the boundaries. The interior points are U = {(a,b): a,b≥2, a+b≤n}. The number of such points is (n-2)(n-3)/2. These points must be covered by the sunny lines as well. So the total number of points that must be covered by the two sunny lines is |U| + 3. Since each sunny line can contain at most floor((n+1)/2) points from the triangle, the total maximum is 2 * floor((n+1)/2). For n≥6, this is at most n+1, but |U|+3 is at least (n-2)(n-3)/2 + 3, which for n=6 is 9 >6, for n=7 is 13 >8, etc. So for n≥6, it's impossible. For n=5, |U|+3 = 3+3=6, and 2 * floor(6/2)=6, so it's possible in terms of count. But we need to check if there exist 2 sunny lines covering all 6 points. We can argue that the only sunny lines that can contain points from U are those with slope 1 (y=x) or slopes that are not 0,∞,-1. But the set U consists of points (2,2),(2,3),(3,2). A line that contains two points from U must be either vertical, horizontal, or slope -1, all non-sunny. So a sunny line can contain at most one point from U. Thus the two sunny lines can cover at most 2 points from U. But we need to cover all 3 points of U. So impossible. Therefore, the configuration with exactly one special line cannot work.

Now, suppose exactly two special lines are present. Without loss, assume x=1 and y=1 are present, but x+y=n+1 is absent. Then we have V≥1, H≥1, D=0. Since we have 2 sunny lines, the total non-sunny lines = n-2. Some of these are vertical, some horizontal, but no diagonal. The vertical edge points are covered by x=1. The horizontal edge points are covered by y=1. The hypotenuse points must be covered by non-diagonal lines (vertical and sunny). Since we have no diagonal lines, we need to cover the hypotenuse points with vertical lines and sunny lines. But vertical lines are only those with x=1 and possibly other vertical lines? The vertical line x=1 covers (1,n). Other vertical lines may cover other points on the hypotenuse. The points on the hypotenuse are (1,n),(2,n-1),...,(n,1). The vertical line x=i covers (i, n+1-i). So if we have vertical lines covering columns 1,...,V, then the points on the hypotenuse that are covered are those with a ≤ V. The remaining points have a = V+1,...,n. These are n-V points. They must be covered by sunny lines. Each sunny line can cover at most 1 point from the hypotenuse (since it's not diagonal). So we need s ≥ n-V. But s=2, so n-V ≤2 => V ≥ n-2. Since V ≤ n-2 (because total non-sunny lines are n-2, and we have H horizontal lines as well), we get V = n-2 and H=0? But we assumed y=1 is present, so H≥1. This is contradictory. Let's do carefully: The total number of non-sunny lines = n-2. They are vertical and horizontal. Let V be the number of vertical lines (including x=1). Let H be the number of horizontal lines (including y=1). Then V+H = n-2. The points on the hypotenuse that are not covered by vertical lines are those with a > V. The number of such points is n-V. Since there are no diagonal lines, these points must be covered by sunny lines. Each sunny line can cover at most 1 point from the hypotenuse. So we need s ≥ n-V. But s=2, so n-V ≤2 => V ≥ n-2. But V ≤ n-2 (since V+H = n-2 and H≥1 => V ≤ n-3). So we get V = n-2 and H=0, but that contradicts the presence of y=1. So the case with exactly two special lines (x=1 and y=1) is impossible.

Similarly, if the two special lines are x=1 and x+y=n+1, but y=1 is absent, then we have V≥1, D≥1, H=0. The horizontal edge points must be covered by non-horizontal lines. The non-horizontal lines are vertical and diagonal and sunny. The horizontal edge points are (a,1). The vertical line x=1 covers (1,1). The diagonal line x+y=n+1 covers (n,1). So the remaining points on the horizontal edge are (2,1) to (n-1,1). There are n-2 points. They must be covered by vertical lines (if any) or sunny lines. Each vertical line x=i covers (i,1) if that point is in the triangle (i ≤ n). So if we have vertical lines covering columns 2,...,V, then the points (V+1,1) to (n-1,1) are uncovered. The diagonal line x+y=n+1 covers (n,1), so (n,1) is covered. So the number of uncovered horizontal edge points is n-1-V (since we have columns 1 to V covered by vertical? Actually, x=1 is present, so column1 covered; if we have other vertical lines, they cover those columns. So the set of columns covered by vertical lines is {1} ∪ some set of size V-1. The uncovered columns are those not in this set. The number of such columns is n - V. These correspond to points (a,1) for a not in the set. That's n-V points. They need to be covered by sunny lines. Since we have 2 sunny lines, each can cover at most 1 point from the horizontal edge, so we need n-V ≤ 2 => V ≥ n-2. But V ≤ n-2 (since total non-sunny = n-2, and D≥1, so V ≤ n-3). Contradiction. So this case impossible.

Similarly, if the two special lines are y=1 and x+y=n+1, symmetric.

Thus, the only possibility for k=2 is to have all three special lines present. So we have x=1, y=1, x+y=n+1 as non-sunny lines. Then we have n-3 non-sunny lines left (since total non-sunny = n-2, we already used 3, so we have n-5 extra non-sunny lines). These extra lines can be vertical, horizontal, or diagonal. We also have 2 sunny lines.

Now, we need to show that this configuration is impossible. We can use a counting argument on the interior points U. The set U = {(a,b): a,b≥2, a+b≤n} has size (n-2)(n-3)/2. The points on the boundary are all covered by the three special lines. The extra non-sunny lines (n-5 of them) can cover some points in U. The sunny lines can also cover points in U. We need to cover all points in U. Let's denote the total points in U that must be covered. Since U is a triangular region, we can bound how many points can be covered by the extra non-sunny lines and the sunny lines. We can use a double counting argument similar to the candidate's: Consider the points on the "diagonal" s = n (i.e., points with a+b = n). There are n-1 such points. They are in U (since a,b≥2). These points must be covered by lines. The only lines that can cover more than one of these points are the diagonal line x+y=n (if present) or possibly other lines? Actually, any line that is not of slope -1 can cover at most one point from this diagonal. So the covering of these points must come from either the diagonal line x+y=n (if present) or from other lines (vertical, horizontal, sunny) each covering at most one point. So if we do not include x+y=n as a line, then we need at least n-1 lines to cover all points on this diagonal. Since we have only n lines total, and we already have 3 special lines, we have n-3 lines left. But n-1 > n-3 for n≥4? For n=5, n-1=4, n-3=2, so we would need 4 lines to cover the diagonal, but we only have 2 lines left. So we must include x+y=n as a non-sunny line. Similarly, consider the points on the diagonal s = n-1 (points with a+b=n-1). There are n-2 such points. They are in U. The only line that can cover more than one is x+y=n-1. So we must include that line as well. Continuing, we can show that we must include all diagonals from s = n+1 down to s = 4? Actually, the points in U have a,b≥2, so the smallest sum is 4. So we need to cover all points on diagonals s=4,...,n. If we don't include the diagonal line x+y=s, then we need at least s-3 lines (since the number of points on that diagonal in U is s-3? Wait, points on diagonal s: (2,s-2),(3,s-3),...,(s-2,2). That's s-3 points. So we would need s-3 lines to cover them. Since we have only n-3 lines left, we need s-3 ≤ n-3 for all s up to n. That's true. But we also need to consider that the same line can cover points on multiple diagonals. So the argument that we must include all diagonals is not immediate.

However, the candidate's argument was to consider the points on the diagonal s=n+1 (hypotenuse) and then s=n, s=n-1, etc., and then use a counting on the number of lines that are not diagonals. They concluded that the only way to cover the points on these diagonals is to use all diagonals except one, and then show that the remaining set of points cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines. That seems to be the approach.

Given the time, I'll adopt a simpler approach: Use a parity or invariant argument. Consider the sum of the coordinates of the points in U modulo something. Or use the fact that any line with slope not 0,∞,-1 can contain at most 1 point from each of the sets {x = constant}, {y = constant}, {x+y = constant}. For U, the points can be arranged in a "triangular grid". The number of points in U is (n-2)(n-3)/2. If we have k=2 sunny lines, they can cover at most 2 * floor((n+1)/2) points from the triangle. But we need to cover U points plus some boundary points? Actually, with all three special lines, the boundary points are covered, so only U needs to be covered. The number of points in U is (n-2)(n-3)/2. For n=5, that's 3; for n=6, 6; for n=7, 10; etc. Each sunny line can cover at most floor((n+1)/2) points from the triangle. For n=6, floor(7/2)=3, so 2 sunny lines cover at most 6 points. That's exactly the size of U. So it's possible for n=6. But we need to check if there exist 2 sunny lines that cover all 6 points of U for n=6. U points: (2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(3,2),(3,3),(4,2). As we saw, the only sunny lines that can contain 2 points from U are those with slope 1? y=x contains (2,2),(3,3) — that's 2 points; y=-0.5x+4 contains (2,3),(4,2) — 2 points; y=-2x+8 contains (2,4),(3,2) — 2 points. So we can have one line covering (2,2),(3,3); another line covering (2,3),(4,2). That leaves (2,4),(3,2) uncovered. We could try to cover them with a third line, but we only have 2. Alternatively, use (2,2)-(4,2) horizontal? Not sunny. So no. Thus k=2 fails for n=6. For n=7, U size=10, 2 sunny lines cover at most 8 points, so impossible. For n=5, U size=3, 2 sunny lines cover at most 6 points, so possible. But we already argued that for n=5, the only way to cover U with 2 sunny lines is to have each cover at most 1 point, so 2 sunny lines cover at most 2 points, but we need 3. So impossible. So k=2 impossible for all n≥3.

Thus the necessary condition is that if k=2, then we must have all three special lines, and then we need to show that the remaining points cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines and the extra non-sunny lines. But note that we have extra non-sunny lines (n-5 of them) that can also cover points in U. So the argument for n=6: we have 3 extra non-sunny lines (since n=6, n-5=1 extra line). For n=7, we have 2 extra non-sunny lines, which can cover, e.g., x=2 and x=3? x=2 covers (2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(2,5); x=3 covers (3,2),(3,3),(3,4). That's 7 points from U, leaving 3 points (4,2),(4,3),(5,2). These 3 points cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines (since no sunny line can contain two of them). So impossible. In general, the number of points in U is (n-2)(n-3)/2. The extra non-sunny lines (n-5) can cover at most (n-5)*(n-3) points? But we need a more precise bound. Thus we have a rigorous argument for k=2. This argument used that we must have all three special lines present. But we need to prove that if k=2, then necessarily x=1, y=1, x+y=n+1 are present. We can argue as follows: Suppose we have a covering with 2 sunny lines. Consider the vertical edge a=1. If x=1 is not present, then V=0. Similarly, if y=1 not present, then H=0. If x+y=n+1 not present, then D=0. So if we avoid all three special lines, we have V=H=D=0, so all lines are sunny, contradicting k=2 (since n>2). So at least one special line is present. Now, suppose exactly one special line is present. Without loss, assume x=1 is present, but y=1 and x+y=n+1 are absent. Then as argued, we must have V≥1 (x=1), H=0, D=0. So the non-sunny lines are all vertical. Let V = n-2. Then we have vertical lines covering n-2 columns. Since we have only 2 sunny lines, we can cover at most 2 points from the horizontal edge. So there are 2 points on the horizontal edge not covered by vertical lines. Those 2 points must be covered by sunny lines. But we have 2 sunny lines, each can cover at most 1 point from that edge, so that's possible. However, we also need to cover the hypotenuse points. The points on the hypotenuse are (a,n+1-a). The vertical lines cover those with a in the set of columns. The uncovered columns are a = V+1,...,n. That's 2 points on the hypotenuse. Those must be covered by sunny lines. Each sunny line can cover at most 1 point from the hypotenuse, so that's possible. So it seems possible that 2 sunny lines cover 2 points on the horizontal edge and 2 points on the hypotenuse, but they might not be the same points. However, we also need to cover the interior points U. The interior points U have size (n-2)(n-3)/2. They must be covered by the sunny lines. But the sunny lines can only cover at most 2 * floor((n+1)/2) points from the triangle. For n≥6, this is at most n+1, while U has size at least (4*3)/2=6 for n=6, and (5*4)/2=10 for n=7, etc. So it's impossible for n≥6. For n=5, U has size 3, and 2 sunny lines can cover at most 6 points, so possible. But we need to cover the interior points with only 2 sunny lines. Thus the case with exactly one special line is impossible.

Now, suppose exactly two special lines are present. Without loss, assume x=1 and y=1 are present, but x+y=n+1 is absent. Then we have V≥1, H≥1, D=0. So non-sunny lines are vertical and horizontal. Let V = number of vertical lines (including x=1), H = number of horizontal lines (including y=1). Then V+H = n-2. The points on the hypotenuse must be covered by non-diagonal lines (vertical and horizontal) and sunny lines. The vertical lines cover points (i, n+1-i) for i in the set of columns. So each line (vertical or horizontal) can cover at most one hypotenuse point. The vertical line x=i covers (i, n+1-i). The horizontal line y=j covers (n+1-j, j). Let's compute the number of hypotenuse points that can be covered by vertical lines: V vertical lines cover V distinct points on the hypotenuse (since each column i gives point (i, n+1-i) if that point is in the triangle; for i from 1 to n, (i,n+1-i) is in the triangle because sum n+1). So V points covered. Similarly, horizontal lines cover H distinct points on the hypotenuse? A horizontal line y=j covers (n+1-j, j). This point is on the hypotenuse if (n+1-j, j) is in the triangle, which is true for j from 1 to n. So each horizontal line covers one hypotenuse point. But note that the vertical line x=1 covers (1,n) and the horizontal line y=1 covers (n,1). These are two different points. The vertical line x=1 and the horizontal line y=1 may overlap? However, these points might be the same if the vertical line x=i and the horizontal line y=j cover the same point? So if we have both a vertical line x=i and a horizontal line y = n+1-i, they cover the same point. But we don't necessarily have that. So the number of distinct hypotenuse points covered by non-sunny lines is at most V+H, but could be less if there is overlap. Since there are n hypotenuse points total, we need at least 2 more lines (sunny lines) to cover the remaining hypotenuse points. The key is that the three special lines are present, but we are in the case where we have only x=1 and y=1, not x+y=n+1. So we have no diagonal lines. The points in U are those with a,b≥2 and a+b≤n. But we have only n-2 non-sunny lines total, with V+H=n-2. The maximum number of points in U that can be covered by these lines is when we choose the columns and rows that cover the most points. The points in U can be partitioned into "strips" of constant a and b. The coverage is complex. However, we can use the following: The set U is a triangular array. The lines x=2, x=3, ..., x=n-2 cover all points in U if we take all vertical lines, but we have only n-2 lines, and we need to cover all points. The best we can do with n-2 lines is to cover all but some points. With 2 sunny lines, we need to cover the rest. This is similar to the earlier analysis for the case with one special line. We can argue that the uncovered points in U after using the n-2 non-sunny lines (with V+H=n-2) will be at least 3 points, and those 3 points cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines (since they are not collinear). But we need to show that no matter how we choose the vertical and horizontal lines, the remaining points cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines.

This is getting messy. Perhaps the candidate's approach of forcing all three special lines is the right one. Let's see the candidate's argument: "Hence, at least one diagonal among s=4,...,n+1 is not used as a non‑sunny line. Let s=k with 4≤k≤n+1 be such a diagonal. The points on s=k are k-1 points. The sunny lines can cover at most two of them, so at least k-3 points must be covered by non‑sunny lines that are not diagonals (i.e., horizontal or vertical lines). Each such non‑sunny line can contain at most one point from this diagonal. Thus we need at least k-3 non‑diagonal non‑sunny lines to cover the points on s=k. However, we have only n-2 non‑sunny lines in total. Among them we have used some diagonals. If we use all diagonals except s=k, that is n-3 diagonals (since there are n-2 diagonals from s=4 to n+1 and we omit one), then we have only 1 non‑diagonal non‑sunny line left. This forces k-3 ≤ 1, i.e., k≤4. Since k≥4, we must have k=4. Hence the only possible omitted diagonal is s=4."

This argument is based on the assumption that the non-sunny lines are exactly the diagonals except possibly some others. But they are not necessarily diagonals; they could be vertical or horizontal. However, the candidate's reasoning seems to be: If we have exactly 2 sunny lines, we must have all three special lines? Actually, they considered the case where exactly 2 sunny lines are used, and then they argued about the diagonals s=4,...,n+1. They assumed that the non-sunny lines are exactly the diagonals? Not exactly; they said "If we use all diagonals except s=k, that is n-3 diagonals (since there are n-2 diagonals from s=4 to n+1 and we omit one)". This suggests that they are considering the possibility that the non-sunny lines are all diagonals (including the hypotenuse). But in the covering with k=2, we have n-2 non-sunny lines. Could they all be diagonals? If we use all diagonals from s=4 to n+1, that's (n+1)-4+1 = n-2 diagonals. So that is a possible configuration: take all diagonals s=4,5,...,n+1 as non-sunny lines. Then we have exactly 2 sunny lines. In this configuration, the points on the diagonals are covered by the diagonals. The points with sum 2 and 3 are not covered by diagonals. Those points are (1,1) (sum2) and (1,2),(2,1) (sum3). Those are 3 points. They must be covered by the 2 sunny lines. But these 3 points are not collinear on a sunny line. So this configuration fails. So the candidate considered that we might have some other combination of non-sunny lines, not all diagonals. They argued that if we omit one diagonal, then we need to cover the points on that diagonal with non-diagonal non-sunny lines (vertical/horizontal). They then count the number of such lines needed. This is a more systematic argument.

Let's try to reconstruct that argument for general n:

We have n lines total. Exactly k sunny lines. We need to cover all points. Consider the points with a+b = s for s from 2 to n+1. For each such diagonal, the points are (1,s-1),(2,s-2),...,(s-1,1). The number of points on diagonal s is s-1.

Now, we have non-sunny lines: vertical, horizontal, diagonal. They can cover points on these diagonals. For a given diagonal s, the only lines that can cover more than one point on that diagonal are the diagonal lines x+y=s (since any other line can intersect that diagonal at most once). So if we do not use the diagonal line x+y=s as a non-sunny line, then all points on that diagonal must be covered by other lines (vertical, horizontal, sunny). Each such line can cover at most one point from that diagonal. So we need at least s-1 lines to cover the points on diagonal s if we do not use the diagonal line.

Now, we have k sunny lines. They can cover at most k points from any given diagonal? Actually, each sunny line can cover at most 1 point from a given diagonal (since slope not -1). So they contribute at most k points. The other non-sunny lines (vertical and horizontal) can also cover at most 1 point each from that diagonal. So the total number of lines that can cover points on diagonal s is n (total lines) minus the number of lines that are parallel to the diagonal? Actually, any line that is not the diagonal line x+y=s can cover at most one point from that diagonal. So the maximum number of points we can cover from diagonal s is the number of lines that are not the diagonal line. That is n - (number of diagonal lines that are exactly x+y=s). If we do not include x+y=s as a line, then all n lines can cover at most one point each, so we need at least s-1 lines to cover s-1 points, which is satisfied if n ≥ s-1. So no immediate contradiction.

However, the candidate's argument considered the set of all non-sunny lines, and assumed that they are all diagonals except possibly some others. They tried to count how many diagonal lines are used. The key is that the total number of non-sunny lines is n-k. They considered the possibility that some of these are diagonals. They argued that if we have a diagonal that is not used, then we need to cover its points with non-diagonal lines, and the number of such points is s-1, and we need at least s-1 non-diagonal lines. But we have only n-k non-sunny lines in total. This leads to an inequality. The candidate's reasoning: "If we use all diagonals except s=k, that is n-3 diagonals (since there are n-2 diagonals from s=4 to n+1 and we omit one), then we have only 1 non‑diagonal non‑sunny line left. Given the time, I'll produce a refined proof that uses the candidate's reasoning but with a clear explanation of why we need to use all diagonals. I'll write:

**Lemma: For any covering of P with n lines, the number k of sunny lines satisfies k ≤ 3.**

Proof: Suppose k ≥ 4. Consider the points on the diagonals s=4,5,...,n+1. The total number of such points is S = n(n+1)/2 - 3. Now, each sunny line can contain at most one point from each such diagonal. Hence, the total number of points from these diagonals that can be covered by the k sunny lines is at most k * (n-2) = k(n-2). The non-sunny lines are at most n-k in number. A non-sunny line can contain at most n points from these diagonals if it is the diagonal line x+y=n+1, but if it is a vertical or horizontal line, it can contain at most n-2 points. However, note that the non-sunny lines must also cover the points on the diagonals s=2,3. So the maximum number of points from the diagonals s=4,...,n+1 that can be covered by the non-sunny lines is at most (n-k)*(n-2). This is because the best a non-sunny line can do for these diagonals is to be a vertical or horizontal line covering n-2 points, and we have n-k such lines. Thus, the total number of points from these diagonals that can be covered is at most k(n-2) + (n-k)(n-2) = n(n-2). For n≥3, n(n-2) is less than n(n+1)/2 - 3? Let's check n=5: n(n-2)=15, S=12, 15≥12, so not less. For n=6: 24, S=18, 24≥18. For n=7: 35, S=28, 35≥28. So no contradiction. But we can get a stronger bound: The non-sunny lines, being of types vertical/horizontal/diagonal, cannot all cover n-2 points from A because they also cover points on s=2,3. In fact, the maximum number of points from A that a non-sunny line can cover is n-2 (if it is vertical or horizontal) or n (if it is the diagonal x+y=n+1). But if it is the diagonal, it covers n points from A, but that's only one line. So if we have many non-sunny lines, we might use the diagonal line to cover many points. But we are assuming k≥4, so n-k ≤ n-4. The total number of points from A that can be covered by the non-sunny lines is at most (n-k)*n (if all are diagonals). That gives n(n-k) + k(n-2) = n^2 - 2k. For k≥4, this is at least n^2 - 8. For n=5, 17, S=12, okay. So no contradiction.

Thus, I think the only way to get a contradiction is to use the fact that the points on the diagonals s=4,5,...,n+1 are not all independent; they form a triangular grid, and the lines that cover many points must be diagonals. The candidate's argument for k=2 used that we need to cover the points on the diagonal s=n+1 with n points, and we only have 2 sunny lines, so we need to use the diagonal line. Similarly, for k≥4, we can argue that we need to use the diagonal lines for all s from 4 to n+1 because otherwise we would need many sunny lines to cover the points on those diagonals. But we have only k sunny lines, and each sunny line can cover at most 1 point from each diagonal. So if we have k sunny lines, they can cover at most k points from each diagonal. The diagonal s=n+1 has n points. If we don't use x+y=n+1, we need to cover n points with k sunny lines and the non-sunny lines. But the non-sunny lines can cover at most 1 point each from this diagonal. So the total number of points that can be covered from this diagonal is at most k + (n-k) = n. So it's possible to cover all n points if each line covers exactly one point. So no need to use the diagonal line. However, we also need to cover the points on the diagonal s=n. There are n-1 points. If we don't use x+y=n, we need to cover them. The same lines can cover points on both diagonals. For example, a vertical line x=1 covers (1,n) on hypotenuse and (1,n-1) on s=n.
